	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >Supernova</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Star exploding at the end of its stellar evolution</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Supernova_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Supernova (disambiguation)">Supernova (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SN1994D.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/260px-SN1994D.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/390px-SN1994D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/520px-SN1994D.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SN1994D.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SN_1994D" title="SN 1994D">SN 1994D</a> (bright spot on the lower left), a <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">type Ia supernova</a> within its host galaxy, <a href="/wiki/NGC_4526" title="NGC 4526">NGC 4526</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>supernova</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/ər/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">ər</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span> plural: <b>supernovae</b> <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/ər/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">ər</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <b>supernovas</b>, abbreviations: <b>SN</b> and <b>SNe</b>) is a powerful and luminous stellar <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosion</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Transient_astronomical_event" class="mw-redirect" title="Transient astronomical event">transient astronomical event</a> occurs during the last <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolutionary stages</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Massive_star" class="mw-redirect" title="Massive star">massive star</a> or when a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> is triggered into runaway <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. The original object, called the <i>progenitor</i>, either collapses to a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a> or <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>, or is completely destroyed. The peak optical <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a> of a supernova can be comparable to that of an entire <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> before fading over several weeks or months.
</p><p>Supernovae are more energetic than <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, <i>nova</i> means "new", referring astronomically to what appears to be a temporary new bright star. Adding the prefix "super-" distinguishes supernovae from ordinary novae, which are far less luminous. The word <i>supernova</i> was coined by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baade" title="Walter Baade">Walter Baade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> in <a href="/wiki/1929" title="1929">1929</a>.
</p><p>The most recent directly observed supernova in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> was <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Supernova" title="Kepler&#39;s Supernova">Kepler's Supernova</a> in 1604, but the <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">remnants</a> of more recent supernovae have been found. Observations of supernovae in other galaxies suggest they occur in the Milky Way on average about three times every century. These supernovae would almost certainly be observable with modern astronomical telescopes. The most recent naked-eye supernova was <a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">SN 1987A</a>, the explosion of a <a href="/wiki/Blue_supergiant_star" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue supergiant star">blue supergiant star</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>, a satellite of the Milky Way.
</p><p>Theoretical studies indicate that most supernovae are triggered by one of two basic mechanisms: the sudden re-ignition of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Compact_star" title="Compact star">degenerate star</a> such as a white dwarf, or the sudden <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of a massive star's <a href="/wiki/Stellar_core" title="Stellar core">core</a>. In the first class of events, the object's temperature is raised enough to trigger <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">runaway</a> nuclear fusion, completely disrupting the star. Possible causes are an accumulation of material from a <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary companion</a> through <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Stellar merger">stellar merger</a>. In the massive star case, the core of a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution#Massive_stars" title="Stellar evolution">massive star</a> may undergo sudden collapse, releasing <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational potential energy">gravitational potential energy</a> as a supernova. While some observed supernovae are more complex than these two simplified theories, the astrophysical mechanics are established and accepted by the astronomical community.
</p><p>Supernovae can expel several <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> of material at speeds up to several percent of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. This drives an expanding <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> into the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>, sweeping up an expanding shell of gas and dust observed as a <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a>. Supernovae are a major source of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> in the interstellar medium from <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a>. The expanding shock waves of supernovae can trigger the <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">formation of new stars</a>. Supernova remnants might be a major source of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. Supernovae might produce <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>, though thus far, gravitational waves have been detected only from the mergers of black holes and neutron stars.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_history">Observation history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Observation history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_supernova_observation" title="History of supernova observation">History of supernova observation</a></div>

data-file-width="3864" data-file-height="3864" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Pulsar_wind_nebula" title="Pulsar wind nebula">pulsar wind nebula</a> associated with the <a href="/wiki/1054_supernova" class="mw-redirect" title="1054 supernova">1054 supernova</a>.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_(SN_1054)_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_(%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg/200px-Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="156" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg/300px-Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg/400px-Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1337" data-file-height="1046" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A 1414 text cites a 1055 report: since "the baleful star appeared, a full year has passed and until now its brilliance has not faded."</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Compared to a star's entire history, the visual appearance of a supernova is very brief, sometimes spanning several months, so that the chances of observing one with the naked eye is roughly once in a lifetime. Only a tiny fraction of the 100&#160;billion stars in a typical <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> have the capacity to become a supernova, restricted to either those having large mass or extraordinarily rare kinds of <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary stars</a> containing <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest possible recorded supernova, known as HB9, could have been viewed and recorded by unknown prehistoric people of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> subcontinent, on a rock carving found in Burzahama region in Kashmir, dated to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="2996550000000000000♠"></span>4500<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Before_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Christ">BC</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Joglekar_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joglekar-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/SN_185" title="SN 185">SN 185</a> was viewed by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomers</a> in 185 AD. The brightest recorded supernova was <a href="/wiki/SN_1006" title="SN 1006">SN 1006</a>, which occurred in 1006 AD in the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Lupus_(constellation)" title="Lupus (constellation)">Lupus</a>, and was described by observers across China, Japan, Iraq, Egypt, and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Murdin_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murdin-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-winkler_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winkler-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The widely observed supernova <a href="/wiki/SN_1054" title="SN 1054">SN 1054</a> produced the <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a>. Supernovae <a href="/wiki/SN_1572" title="SN 1572">SN 1572</a> and <a href="/wiki/SN_1604" class="mw-redirect" title="SN 1604">SN 1604</a>, the latest to be observed with the naked eye in the Milky Way galaxy, had notable effects on the development of astronomy in Europe because they were used to argue against the <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> idea that the universe beyond the Moon and planets was static and unchanging.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> began observing SN 1604 at its peak on October 17, 1604, and continued to make estimates of its brightness until it faded from naked eye view a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-kepler_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kepler-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It was the second supernova to be observed in a generation (after SN 1572 seen by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> in Cassiopeia).<sup id="cite_ref-history_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is some evidence that the youngest galactic supernova, <a href="/wiki/G1.9%2B0.3" title="G1.9+0.3">G1.9+0.3</a>, occurred in the late 19th century, considerably more recently than <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_A" title="Cassiopeia A">Cassiopeia A</a> from around 1680.<sup id="cite_ref-chakraborti_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chakraborti-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Neither supernova was noted at the time. In the case of G1.9+0.3, high extinction along the plane of our galaxy could have dimmed the event sufficiently to go unnoticed. The situation for Cassiopeia A is less clear. Infrared <a href="/wiki/Light_echo" title="Light echo">light echos</a> have been detected showing that it was a type IIb supernova and was not in a region of especially high <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)" title="Extinction (astronomy)">extinction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-krause_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krause-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Observation and discovery of extragalactic supernovae are now far more common. The first such observation was of <a href="/wiki/SN_1885A" title="SN 1885A">SN 1885A</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>. Today, amateur and professional astronomers are finding several hundred every year, some when near maximum brightness, others on old astronomical photographs or plates. American astronomers <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_Minkowski" title="Rudolph Minkowski">Rudolph Minkowski</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> developed the modern supernova classification scheme beginning in 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> During the 1960s, astronomers found that the maximum intensities of supernovae could be used as <a href="/wiki/Standard_candles" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard candles">standard candles</a>, hence indicators of astronomical distances.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Some of the most distant supernovae observed in 2003 appeared dimmer than expected. This supports the view that the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Accelerating universe">universe is accelerating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leibundgut_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leibundgut-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Techniques were developed for reconstructing supernovae events that have no written records of being observed. The date of the <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_A" title="Cassiopeia A">Cassiopeia A</a> supernova event was determined from <a href="/wiki/Light_echo" title="Light echo">light echoes</a> off <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> while the age of supernova remnant <a href="/wiki/RX_J0852.0-4622" class="mw-redirect" title="RX J0852.0-4622">RX J0852.0-4622</a> was estimated from temperature measurements<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> emissions from the radioactive decay of <a href="/wiki/Titanium-44" class="mw-redirect" title="Titanium-44">titanium-44</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:One_galaxy,_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg/220px-One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg/330px-One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg/440px-One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4247" data-file-height="3679" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:One_galaxy,_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SN Antikythera in galaxy cluster RXC J0949.8+1707. SN Eleanor and SN Alexander were observed in the same galaxy in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The most luminous supernova ever recorded is <a href="/wiki/ASASSN-15lh" title="ASASSN-15lh">ASASSN-15lh</a>, at a distance of 3.82 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">gigalight-years</a>. It was first detected in June 2015 and peaked at 570 billion&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity"><var>L</var><sub>&#x2609;</sub></a>, which is twice the <a href="/wiki/Bolometric_luminosity" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolometric luminosity">bolometric luminosity</a> of any other known supernova.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> However, the nature of this supernova continues to be debated and several alternative explanations have been suggested, e.g. tidal disruption of a star by a black hole.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the earliest detected since time of detonation, and for which the earliest spectra have been obtained (beginning at 6 hours after the actual explosion), is the type II <a href="/wiki/SN_2013fs" title="SN 2013fs">SN 2013fs</a> (iPTF13dqy) which was recorded 3 hours after the supernova event on 6 October 2013 by the <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Transient_Factory" title="Palomar Transient Factory">Intermediate Palomar Transient Factory</a> (iPTF). The star is located in a <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral galaxy</a> named <a href="/wiki/NGC_7610" title="NGC 7610">NGC 7610</a>, 160&#160;million light-years away in the constellation of Pegasus.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 20 September 2016, amateur astronomer Victor Buso from <a href="/wiki/Rosario" class="mw-redirect" title="Rosario">Rosario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> was testing his telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-AS_Now_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NatBuso_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatBuso-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> When taking several photographs of galaxy <a href="/wiki/NGC_613" title="NGC 613">NGC 613</a>, Buso chanced upon a supernova that had just become visible on Earth. After examining the images, he contacted the Instituto de Astrofísica de La Plata. "It was the first time anyone had ever captured the initial moments of the 'shock breakout' from an optical supernova, one not associated with a gamma-ray or X-ray burst."<sup id="cite_ref-AS_Now_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The odds of capturing such an event were put between one in ten million to one in a hundred million, according to astronomer Melina Bersten from the Instituto de Astrofísica.
The supernova Buso observed was a type IIb made by a star twenty times the mass of the  sun.<sup id="cite_ref-AS_Now_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Alex_Filippenko" title="Alex Filippenko">Alex Filippenko</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California" title="University of California">University of California</a>, remarked that professional astronomers had been searching for such an event for a long time. He stated: "Observations of stars in the first moments they begin exploding provide information that cannot be directly obtained in any other way."<sup id="cite_ref-AS_Now_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_supernova_observation#Telescope_observation" title="History of supernova observation">History of supernova observation §&#160;Telescope observation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg/220px-NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg/330px-NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg/440px-NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="3600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">Supernova remnant</a> SNR E0519-69.0 in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a></div></div></div>
<p>Early work on what was originally believed to be simply a new category of <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a> was performed during the 1920s. These were variously called "upper-class Novae", "Hauptnovae", or "giant novae".<sup id="cite_ref-bode_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bode-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The name "supernovae" is thought to have been coined by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baade" title="Walter Baade">Walter Baade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> in lectures at <a href="/wiki/Caltech" class="mw-redirect" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> during 1931. It was used, as "super-Novae", in a journal paper published by <a href="/wiki/Knut_Lundmark" title="Knut Lundmark">Knut Lundmark</a> in 1933,<sup id="cite_ref-baas33_1330_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baas33_1330-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and in a 1934 paper by Baade and Zwicky.<sup id="cite_ref-Baade1934_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baade1934-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> By 1938, the hyphen had been lost and the modern name was in use.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Because supernovae are relatively rare events within a galaxy, occurring about three times a century in the Milky Way,<sup id="cite_ref-reynolds_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reynolds-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> obtaining a good sample of supernovae to study requires regular monitoring of many galaxies.
</p><p>Supernovae in other galaxies cannot be predicted with any meaningful accuracy. Normally, when they are discovered, they are already in progress.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> To use supernovae as <a href="/wiki/Standard_candle" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard candle">standard candles</a> for measuring distance, observation of their peak luminosity is required. It is therefore important to discover them well before they reach their maximum. <a href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomy" title="Amateur astronomy">Amateur astronomers</a>, who greatly outnumber professional astronomers, have played an important role in finding supernovae, typically by looking at some of the closer galaxies through an <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">optical telescope</a> and comparing them to earlier photographs.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toward the end of the 20th century, astronomers increasingly turned to computer-controlled telescopes and <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCDs</a> for hunting supernovae. While such systems are popular with amateurs, there are also professional installations such as the <a href="/wiki/Katzman_Automatic_Imaging_Telescope" title="Katzman Automatic Imaging Telescope">Katzman Automatic Imaging Telescope</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Supernova_Early_Warning_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Supernova Early Warning System">Supernova Early Warning System</a> (SNEWS) project uses a network of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_detector" title="Neutrino detector">neutrino detectors</a> to give early warning of a supernova in the Milky Way galaxy.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">Neutrinos</a> are <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particles</a> that are produced in great quantities by a supernova, and they are not significantly absorbed by the interstellar gas and dust of the galactic disk.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_star_set_to_explode.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/A_star_set_to_explode.jpg/260px-A_star_set_to_explode.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/A_star_set_to_explode.jpg/390px-A_star_set_to_explode.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/A_star_set_to_explode.jpg/520px-A_star_set_to_explode.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3955" data-file-height="2008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_star_set_to_explode.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"A star set to explode", the SBW1 nebula surrounds a massive blue supergiant in the <a href="/wiki/Carina_Nebula" title="Carina Nebula">Carina Nebula</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Supernova searches fall into two classes: those focused on relatively nearby events and those looking farther away. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric expansion of space">expansion of the universe</a>, the distance to a remote object with a known <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission spectrum</a> can be estimated by measuring its <a href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" class="mw-redirect" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>); on average, more-distant objects recede with greater velocity than those nearby, and so have a higher redshift. Thus the search is split between high redshift and low redshift, with the boundary falling around a redshift range of <i>z</i>=0.1–0.3<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>—where <i>z</i> is a dimensionless measure of the spectrum's frequency shift.
</p><p>High redshift searches for supernovae usually involve the observation of supernova light curves. These are useful for standard or calibrated candles to generate <a href="/wiki/Hubble_diagram" class="mw-redirect" title="Hubble diagram">Hubble diagrams</a> and make cosmological predictions. Supernova spectroscopy, used to study the physics and environments of supernovae, is more practical at low than at high redshift.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Low redshift observations also anchor the low-distance end of the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Hubble curve">Hubble curve</a>, which is a plot of distance versus redshift for visible galaxies.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_convention">Naming convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naming convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Keplers_supernova.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Keplers_supernova.jpg/220px-Keplers_supernova.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Keplers_supernova.jpg/330px-Keplers_supernova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Keplers_supernova.jpg/440px-Keplers_supernova.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Keplers_supernova.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Multi-wavelength <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Optical" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical">optical</a> compilation image of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler's</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1604" class="mw-redirect" title="SN 1604">SN 1604</a></div></div></div><p>Supernova discoveries are reported to the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Central_Bureau_for_Astronomical_Telegrams" title="Central Bureau for Astronomical Telegrams">Central Bureau for Astronomical Telegrams</a>, which sends out a circular with the name it assigns to that supernova. The name is formed from the prefix <i>SN</i>, followed by the year of discovery, suffixed with a one or two-letter designation. The first 26 supernovae of the year are designated with a capital letter from <i>A</i> to <i>Z</i>. Afterward pairs of lower-case letters are used: <i>aa</i>, <i>ab</i>, and so on. Hence, for example, <i>SN 2003C</i> designates the third supernova reported in the year 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The last supernova of 2005, SN 2005nc, was the 367th (14 × 26 + 3 = 367). The suffix "nc" acts as a <a href="/wiki/Bijective_numeration#The_bijective_base-26_system" title="Bijective numeration">bijective base-26</a> encoding, with <i>a</i> = 1, <i>b</i> = 2, <i>c</i> = 3, ... <i>z</i> = 26. Since 2000, professional and amateur astronomers have been finding several hundred supernovae each year (572 in 2007, 261 in 2008, 390 in 2009; 231 in 2013).<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historical supernovae are known simply by the year they occurred: <a href="/wiki/SN_185" title="SN 185">SN 185</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1006" title="SN 1006">SN 1006</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1054" title="SN 1054">SN 1054</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1572" title="SN 1572">SN 1572</a> (called <i>Tycho's Nova</i>) and <a href="/wiki/SN_1604" class="mw-redirect" title="SN 1604">SN 1604</a> (<i>Kepler's Star</i>). Since 1885 the additional letter notation has been used, even if there was only one supernova discovered that year (e.g. <a href="/wiki/SN_1885A" title="SN 1885A">SN 1885A</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1907A&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SN 1907A (page does not exist)">SN 1907A</a>, etc.)—this last happened with <a href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1947A&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SN 1947A (page does not exist)">SN 1947A</a>. <i>SN</i>, for SuperNova, is a standard prefix. Until 1987, two-letter designations were rarely needed; since 1988, however, they have been needed every year. Since 2016, the increasing number of discoveries has regularly led to the additional use of three-digit designations.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of supernova 1993J<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Astronomers classify supernovae according to their <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curves</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Absorption_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Absorption line">absorption lines</a> of different <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> that appear in their <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectra</a>. If a supernova's spectrum contains lines of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Balmer_series" title="Balmer series">Balmer series</a> in the visual portion of the spectrum) it is classified <i>Type II</i>; otherwise it is <i>Type I</i>. In each of these two types there are subdivisions according to the presence of lines from other elements or the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> (a graph of the supernova's <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> as a function of time).<sup id="cite_ref-types_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-types-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taxonomy_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_I">Type I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Type I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Type I supernovae are subdivided on the basis of their spectra, with type Ia showing a strong <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">ionised silicon</a> <a href="/wiki/Absorption_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Absorption line">absorption line</a>. Type I supernovae without this strong line are classified as type Ib and Ic, with type Ib showing strong neutral helium lines and type Ic lacking them. The light curves are all similar, although type Ia are generally brighter at peak luminosity, but the light curve is not important for classification of type I supernovae.
</p><p>A small number of type Ia supernovae exhibit unusual features, such as non-standard luminosity or broadened light curves, and these are typically classified by referring to the earliest example showing similar features. For example, the sub-luminous <a href="/wiki/SN_2008ha" title="SN 2008ha">SN 2008ha</a> is often referred to as <a href="/wiki/SN_2002cx" title="SN 2002cx">SN 2002cx</a>-like or class Ia-2002cx.
</p><p>A small proportion of type Ic supernovae show highly broadened and blended emission lines which are taken to indicate very high expansion velocities for the ejecta. These have been classified as type Ic-BL or Ic-bl.<sup id="cite_ref-bianco_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bianco-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_II">Type II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Type II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SNIIcurva.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/SNIIcurva.svg/220px-SNIIcurva.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/SNIIcurva.svg/330px-SNIIcurva.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/SNIIcurva.svg/440px-SNIIcurva.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1052" data-file-height="744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SNIIcurva.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Light curves are used to classify type II-P and type II-L supernovae.</div></div></div>
<p>The supernovae of <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">type II</a> can also be sub-divided based on their spectra. While most type II supernovae show very broad <a href="/wiki/Emission_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Emission line">emission lines</a> which indicate expansion velocities of many thousands of <a href="/wiki/Kilometres_per_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilometres per second">kilometres per second</a>, some, such as <a href="/wiki/SN_2005gl" title="SN 2005gl">SN 2005gl</a>, have relatively narrow features in their spectra. These are called type IIn, where the 'n' stands for 'narrow'.
</p><p>A few supernovae, such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1987K&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SN 1987K (page does not exist)">SN 1987K</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/SN_1993J" title="SN 1993J">SN 1993J</a>, appear to change types: they show lines of hydrogen at early times, but, over a period of weeks to months, become dominated by lines of helium. The term <a href="/wiki/Type_IIb_supernova" class="mw-redirect" title="Type IIb supernova">"type IIb"</a> is used to describe the combination of features normally associated with types II and Ib.<sup id="cite_ref-taxonomy_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Type II supernovae with normal spectra dominated by broad hydrogen lines that remain for the life of the decline are classified on the basis of their light curves. The most common type shows a distinctive "plateau" in the light curve shortly after peak brightness where the visual luminosity stays relatively constant for several months before the decline resumes. These are called type II-P referring to the plateau. Less common are type II-L supernovae that lack a distinct plateau. The "L" signifies "linear" although the light curve is not actually a straight line.
</p><p>Supernovae that do not fit into the normal classifications are designated peculiar, or 'pec'.<sup id="cite_ref-taxonomy_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Types_III.2C_IV.2C_and_V"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_III,_IV,_and_V">Types III, IV, and V</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Types III, IV, and V">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> defined additional supernovae types based on a very few examples that did not cleanly fit the parameters for type I or type II supernovae. <a href="/wiki/SN_1961i" class="mw-redirect" title="SN 1961i">SN 1961i</a> in <a href="/wiki/NGC_4303" class="mw-redirect" title="NGC 4303">NGC 4303</a> was the prototype and only member of the type III supernova class, noted for its broad light curve maximum and broad hydrogen Balmer lines that were slow to develop in the spectrum. <a href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1961f&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SN 1961f (page does not exist)">SN 1961f</a> in <a href="/wiki/NGC_3003" title="NGC 3003">NGC 3003</a> was the prototype and only member of the type IV class, with a light curve similar to a type II-P supernova, with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_absorption_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen absorption line">hydrogen absorption lines</a> but weak <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_emission_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen emission line">hydrogen emission lines</a>. The type V class was coined for <a href="/wiki/SN_1961V" title="SN 1961V">SN 1961V</a> in <a href="/wiki/NGC_1058" title="NGC 1058">NGC 1058</a>, an unusual faint supernova or <a href="/wiki/Supernova_impostor" title="Supernova impostor">supernova impostor</a> with a slow rise to brightness, a maximum lasting many months, and an unusual emission spectrum. The similarity of SN 1961V to the <a href="/wiki/Eta_Carinae" title="Eta Carinae">Eta Carinae</a> Great Outburst was noted.<sup id="cite_ref-zwicky2_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zwicky2-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Supernovae in M101 (1909) and M83 (1923 and 1957) were also suggested as possible type IV or type V supernovae.<sup id="cite_ref-zwicky_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zwicky-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These types would now all be treated as peculiar type II supernovae (IIpec), of which many more examples have been discovered, although it is still debated whether SN 1961V was a true supernova following an <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBV</a> outburst or an impostor.<sup id="cite_ref-doggett_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_models">Current models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Current models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg/260px-The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg/390px-The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg/520px-The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1580" data-file-height="664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the galaxy <a href="/wiki/NGC_1365" title="NGC 1365">NGC 1365</a> a supernova (the bright dot slightly above the galactic center) rapidly brightens, then fades more slowly.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Supernovae type codes, as described above, are <i><a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomic</a></i>: the type number describes the light observed from the supernova, not necessarily its cause. For example, type Ia supernovae are produced by runaway fusion ignited on degenerate <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> progenitors, while the spectrally similar type Ib/c are produced from massive Wolf–Rayet progenitors by core collapse. The following summarises what is currently believed to be the most plausible explanations for supernovae.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_runaway">Thermal runaway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Thermal runaway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia supernova</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg/220px-Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg/330px-Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg/440px-Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="742" data-file-height="761" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Formation of a type Ia supernova</div></div></div>
<p>A white dwarf star may accumulate sufficient material from a <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">stellar companion</a> to raise its core temperature enough to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_detonation" title="Carbon detonation">ignite</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_burning_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon burning process">carbon fusion</a>, at which point it undergoes <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">runaway</a> nuclear fusion, completely disrupting it. There are three avenues by which this detonation is theorised to happen: stable <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> of material from a companion, the collision of two white dwarfs, or accretion that causes ignition in a shell that then ignites the core. The dominant mechanism by which type Ia supernovae are produced remains unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-Piro2014_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piro2014-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Despite this uncertainty in how type Ia supernovae are produced, type Ia supernovae have very uniform properties and are useful standard candles over intergalactic distances. Some calibrations are required to compensate for the gradual change in properties or different frequencies of abnormal luminosity supernovae at high redshift, and for small variations in brightness identified by light curve shape or spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-chen_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chen-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Normal_Type_Ia">Normal Type Ia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Normal Type Ia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are several means by which a supernova of this type can form, but they share a common underlying mechanism. If a <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>-<a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> accreted enough matter to reach the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a> of about 1.44 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#x2609;</sub></a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Mazzali2007_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzali2007-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> (for a non-rotating star), it would no longer be able to support the bulk of its mass through <a href="/wiki/Electron_degeneracy_pressure" title="Electron degeneracy pressure">electron degeneracy pressure</a><sup id="cite_ref-Chandrasekhar_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chandrasekhar-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-canal1997_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canal1997-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and would begin to collapse. However, the current view is that this limit is not normally attained; increasing temperature and density inside the core <a href="/wiki/Carbon_detonation" title="Carbon detonation">ignite</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_burning_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon burning process">carbon fusion</a> as the star approaches the limit (to within about 1%<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>) before collapse is initiated.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazzali2007_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzali2007-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  For a core primarily composed of oxygen, neon and magnesium, the collapsing white dwarf will typically form a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>. In this case, only a fraction of the star's mass will be ejected during the collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-canal1997_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canal1997-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within a few seconds, a substantial fraction of the matter in the white dwarf undergoes nuclear fusion, releasing enough energy (1–<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7044200000000000000♠"></span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>44</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-aaa270_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaa270-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_binding_energy" title="Gravitational binding energy">unbind</a> the star in a supernova.<sup id="cite_ref-ropke_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ropke-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> An outwardly expanding <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> is generated, with matter reaching velocities on the order of 5,000–20,000 <a href="/wiki/Kilometers_per_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilometers per second">km/s</a>, or roughly 3% of the speed of light. There is also a significant increase in luminosity, reaching an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of −19.3 (or 5&#160;billion times brighter than the Sun), with little variation.<sup id="cite_ref-explosion_model_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-explosion_model-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The model for the formation of this category of supernova is a close <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> system. The larger of the two stars is the first to <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolve</a> off the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>, and it expands to form a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>. The two stars now share a common envelope, causing their mutual orbit to shrink. The giant star then sheds most of its envelope, losing mass until it can no longer continue <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. At this point, it becomes a white dwarf star, composed primarily of carbon and oxygen.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Eventually, the secondary star also evolves off the main sequence to form a red giant. Matter from the giant is accreted by the white dwarf, causing the latter to increase in mass. Despite widespread acceptance of the basic model, the exact details of initiation and of the heavy elements produced in the catastrophic event are still unclear.
</p><p>Type Ia supernovae follow a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a>—the graph of luminosity as a function of time—after the event. This luminosity is generated by the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>-56 through <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>-56 to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>-56.<sup id="cite_ref-explosion_model_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-explosion_model-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The peak luminosity of the light curve is extremely consistent across normal type Ia supernovae, having a maximum <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of about −19.3. This is because type Ia supernovae arise from a consistent type of progenitor star by gradual mass acquisition, and explode when they acquire a consistent typical mass, giving rise to very similar supernova conditions and behavior. This allows them to be used as a secondary<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Standard_candle" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard candle">standard candle</a> to measure the distance to their host galaxies.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-standard_Type_Ia">Non-standard Type Ia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Non-standard Type Ia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another model for the formation of type Ia supernovae involves the merger of two white dwarf stars, with the combined mass momentarily exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> There is much variation in this type of event,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and, in many cases, there may be no supernova at all, in which case they will have a broader and less luminous light curve than the more normal SN type Ia.
</p><p>Abnormally bright type Ia supernovae occur when the white dwarf already has a mass higher than the Chandrasekhar limit,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> possibly enhanced further by asymmetry,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> but the ejected material will have less than normal kinetic energy.
</p><p>There is no formal sub-classification for the non-standard type Ia supernovae. It has been proposed that a group of sub-luminous supernovae that occur when helium accretes onto a white dwarf should be classified as <b>type Iax</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-wang_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wang-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-foley_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foley-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This type of supernova may not always completely destroy the white dwarf progenitor and could leave behind a <a href="/wiki/Zombie_star" title="Zombie star">zombie star</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mccully_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mccully-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One specific type of non-standard type Ia supernova develops hydrogen, and other, emission lines and gives the appearance of mixture between a normal type Ia and a type IIn supernova. Examples are <a href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2002ic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SN 2002ic (page does not exist)">SN 2002ic</a> and <a href="/wiki/SN_2005gj" title="SN 2005gj">SN 2005gj</a>. These supernovae have been dubbed <b>type Ia/IIn</b>, <b>type Ian</b>, <b>type IIa</b> and <b>type IIan</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_collapse">Core collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Core collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/220px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/330px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/440px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="550" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The layers of a massive, evolved star just before core collapse (not to scale)</div></div></div>
<p>Very massive stars can undergo core collapse when nuclear fusion becomes unable to sustain the core against its own gravity; passing this threshold is the cause of all types of supernova except type Ia. The collapse may cause violent expulsion of the outer layers of the star resulting in a supernova, or the release of gravitational potential energy may be insufficient and the star may collapse into a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a> with little radiated energy.
</p><p>Core collapse can be caused by several different mechanisms: exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a>; <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pair-instability_supernova" title="Pair-instability supernova">pair-instability</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Photodisintegration" title="Photodisintegration">photodisintegration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-heger_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heger-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nomoto_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nomoto-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>When a massive star develops an iron core larger than the Chandrasekhar mass it will no longer be able to support itself by <a href="/wiki/Electron_degeneracy_pressure" title="Electron degeneracy pressure">electron degeneracy pressure</a> and will collapse further to a neutron star or black hole.</li>
<li>Electron capture by magnesium in a <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_star" class="mw-redirect" title="Degenerate star">degenerate</a> O/Ne/Mg core causes <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> followed by explosive oxygen fusion, with very similar results.</li>
<li>Electron-positron pair production in a large post-helium burning core removes thermodynamic support and causes initial collapse followed by runaway fusion, resulting in a pair-instability supernova.</li>
<li>A sufficiently large and hot <a href="/wiki/Stellar_core" title="Stellar core">stellar core</a> may generate gamma-rays energetic enough to initiate photodisintegration directly, which will cause a complete collapse of the core.</li></ul>
<p>The table below lists the known reasons for core collapse in massive stars, the types of stars in which they occur, their associated supernova type, and the remnant produced. The <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> is the proportion of elements other than hydrogen or helium, as compared to the Sun. The initial mass is the mass of the star prior to the supernova event, given in multiples of the Sun's mass, although the mass at the time of the supernova may be much lower.
</p><p>Type IIn supernovae are not listed in the table. They can be produced by various types of core collapse in different progenitor stars, possibly even by type Ia white dwarf ignitions, although it seems that most will be from iron core collapse in luminous <a href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">supergiants</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hypergiant" title="Hypergiant">hypergiants</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBVs</a>). The narrow spectral lines for which they are named occur because the supernova is expanding into a small dense cloud of circumstellar material.<sup id="cite_ref-moriya_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moriya-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It appears that a significant proportion of supposed type IIn supernovae are <a href="/wiki/Supernova_impostor" title="Supernova impostor">supernova impostors</a>, massive eruptions of <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBV</a>-like stars similar to the Great Eruption of <a href="/wiki/Eta_Carinae" title="Eta Carinae">Eta Carinae</a>. In these events, material previously ejected from the star creates the narrow absorption lines and causes a shock wave through interaction with the newly ejected material.<sup id="cite_ref-smith_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
